That's SO AMERICAN... mixing metric/imperial...  we use metric for liquids (often), medicine (always), food labels (always/often both) etc...  We mostly use imperial for distances (miles, feet, inches) and liquids at times (Gallon, Ounce) but near everything else is in metric (mostly). In everyday use many will float between them... e.g. I wants to weight m food... often I'll do it in grams for precision but things like meat (chicken, beef) we'll often use ounces. It just depends, but I think you'd be surpised how much we use metric.

Looks awful
a little late to this, but the ounces referred to by the machine are fluid ounces, which are different from weight or mass ounces, which are the smallest normal imperial mass unit. Anything with a mass typically lower or around an ounce is almost always measured in grams (like American which funny enough often put servings in oz and ml but, always use grams/mg for nutrition). Something like coffee would be painful in ounces because most measurements be a fraction of an oz (1 oz ~ 28g), and metric can go down to mg, much more useful in small measurements (plus the fact imperial has to different kinds of ounces that don't correlate and cant measure the same thing is stupid and confusing, we only really use mass ounces for meat and large stuff cause big American hands).

Water in ounces and coffee in grams makes sense because (A) water is basically a constant density in normal conditions, meaning fluid ounces of water are very consistent measurements (as compared to say, cups of flour), (B) every American has a measuring pyrex spout cup with fluid ounce markers, (C) coffee volume varies by contrast so weight measurements are better, and (D) A lot of people are confused by the difference between fluid ounces (so keeping consistent measurements between coffee/water would be confusing anyway) and weight ounces but grams on a digital scale is pretty unambiguous.

I wonder if part of the problem may be audience. You know what good coffee is supposed to taste like, and how to make it. But this thing isn't, I don't think, intended to compete against V60s and Aeropresses and $300 cups of coffee. It's intended to compete with Mr. Coffee and Maxwell House. It gives people access to whole beans in a way that's convenient, and if it sacrifices quality to an extent that's okay because frankly anyone who drinks Folgers probably isn't noticing them anyway (full disclosure, I frequently drink Folgers, because it's cheap and available on construction sites).   Would this work for a leading expert in the coffee field? No, absolutely not. Obviously. Would this improve the quality of coffee available to a busy family as one parent goes off to work and the other rushes to drop the kids off for school? That question is, I think, still unanswered. Studying how this is not only functionally designed but also how it is being marketed helps reveal a lot about the business process that created it and who it was intended to be sold to. The biggest indicator is how (in the fine print!) the quality is being directly compared with the Keurig. Those machines (and their competitors) are overwhelmingly sold on their easy of use for making a single cup of coffee at a time, something intended more for shared spaces like an office. Someone brewing at home will care about this less; shared space coffee machines don''t easily store excess brewed coffee without a lot of extra fuss & equipment.  The challenge of cleaning this machine will be its achilles heel. Clearly, the designers understand the challenge and have tried to make it easier to disassemble. But that's not going to be enough. The ease of cleaning up a brew setup directly influences my choices in how I make coffee. For a good quality cup (or carafe), I am willing to work with the messiness of a french press, but I usually opt for a simpler pour-over or auto-drip machine. Yes, I can taste the quality difference, and all things being equal I much prefer a good quality immersion brew. But not all things are equal: I prefer systems that keeps the coffee in a neatly contained paper filter. I also highly dislike pod-based systems like Nespresso or Keurig because of the increased use and exposure to plastics (and yes, it does impact the flavor).  Which brings me to the user interface. I've worked in offices where there was a large bean-to-brew machine to grind and make coffee on demand. It clearly was a complex and expensive piece of equipment that took a lot of effort to maintain. For a large office with dozens of people, one can make an argument about its cost-effectiveness, since the alternatives (pod-based systems not withstanding) are going to have similar scalability issues to a small cafe or restaurant, at which point the real alternative is to just open up a fully independent franchise next door (or otherwise co-located).

This is the problem with celebrity-endorsed products like these. They have a lot of pull in the marketplace because people recognize them, and with recognition comes trust. "Oh, this coffee machine is very cute, and Drew Barrymore made it, so it must be good, right? Surely America's sweetheart wouldn't put her name on something that wasn't good." The company that makes these products (among others), purposely makes a product that ticks certain boxes‚Äîthe grinder suggests a more 'artisanal' cup of coffee similar to what you'd get at a local coffee shop, a bold brew for iced coffee which is likely more popular in the demographic they're targeting (more than likely 25-49 y/o females, specifically busy professionals and/or moms), it's very pretty in a sort of minty sage green and gold, which ties in with the current popular aesthetic of bold kitchen cabinets with gold hardware and mid-mod decor, they used Drew Barrymore who is instantly recognizable by the median target audience of around 35 y/o as they all remember her movies from the 90's and early aughts...  There's a lot of very specific marketing and targeting with this product (as there is with similar products like toasters, air fryers, knives, storage containers, etc.). Selling a functional, but sub-par product at a price that undercuts the competition (which is arguably small because there are very few bean-to-cup systems, if any) to people that likely wouldn't know the difference between a cup of coffee ground by hand and perfectly brewed and a Dunkin K-Cup is pretty brilliant. They'll sell tons of these, and in a few years when the grinder quits working or the filter seizes up or whatever, they'll just end up on shelves, and eventually thrift stores. Meanwhile, Drew will get her royalty, the company will make several-hundred-percent profit on each one sold, and they'll do it all again with another product and another famous person. For Target or Nordstrom or Macy's or whatever.   I'm not surprised this machine wasn't 'better' or even 'good.' It's not meant for people that love good coffee. It's meant for people that love the IDEA of good coffee.

What do you expect from a 99 dollar machine? I expect it was made in the far east, usually the retail price is three times the landed cost which means it has to be manufactured, packaged and shipped to the destination with duty paid.  This cost is then trebled to reach a selling price.  Don‚Äôt forget to get it manufactured they first had to design it, then make mock up models for testing, then it goes into mass production.  The packaging has to pass a drop test, it has to conform to new packing (environmental) rules, the actual brewer needs to pass safety tests for electrical use (on all it components), this is expensive, the box design is expensive and so is shipping, these costs come down if spread over a large production. The celebrity endorsement could be 5% of sales plus an upfront fee. I would guess the actual cost of the machine to be around 20 dollars. What kind of quality can you expect for that?  It would have been so much better to have a decent burr grinder and charge more.  They would have been far wiser to ask James to help with the design and have his face on the box!

America doesn't use Imperial, we use US Customary. Same measurement names, different measurement sizes. Also, Imperial is still used all over the world, ask any Canadian how they set their thermostat or how tall they are, or any brit how much they weigh. But we definitely should be moving the entire world to metric for everything except weather. 0-100F represents the reasonable range of human comfort/survivability. Use centigrade for everything else :)
I would tend to avoid Walmart exclusive products. Even non-exclusive brands you'll often find cheaper at Walmart because Walmart has forced the company to cut back some features, but then put it in the exact same packaging. An example of this is the Ninja Blender System: Walmart's is $10 cheaper than everywhere else but the packaging looks identical. Except it's not, the SKU is different and its motor is only 1500W instead of 1800W. There may be other changes under the hood as well. Walmart has a reputation for selling garbage, because they intentionally sell highly deceptive garbage to undercut competitors.

This is probably the worst review I've seen you do, and I'm not talking about the machine here, I'm talking about you James. Why do you keep trying to use light roasted beans for machines which are clearly aimed at dark roasts?  This isn't the first time you've reviewed something and said something like "if it was used with dark roast beans it would have been much better".  That's like testing a cheap 3D printer designed for PLA using ABS (a much more demanding filament), and complaining that it failed, but saying "it would probably do better with PLA".  At least give the machines a fair chance and test them with beans that suit them. I'm not defending this machine at all, although it's cosmetically appealing, it looks like a pretty awful design from a brewing perspective, and I'm sure it's still going to give bad results, but testing something using beans it wasn't designed for, and then condemning it is hardly scientific, or fair.  You set it up for failure, got what you expected, and then express disappointment.  Thinking about it, I don't think I've ever seen you do any test with a dark roast coffee.  That's a huge disservice to those of us who prefer dark roasts.  Please stop pontificating about how something might fare with dark roasts and actually test it with them.  You may be surprised.  I want to know how something actually performs with dark roast coffee, not your speculation based on how it performs with light roasts.  Just because you like light roasts does not mean everyone does.  Some of us don't drink coffee to analyse it for fruity notes or whether you can discern the terroir like wine, but because we enjoy coffee that tastes of coffee.  Nobody is going to buy a $99 machine to experience the subtleties of specialist coffees.  They want something that'll give them that "wake up and smell the coffee" moment and get better coffee than from a machine that needs pre-ground coffee.  To bring your coffee snobbery to a $99 machine does nobody any good.

The volume of coffee brewed is an issue beyond just the brew ratio. If you have a 350ml/ 12oz mug and you set it to brew 12oz it will fill your mug to the brim and probably overflow, an issue that I believe Keurigs have as well. The solution is then to brew 11oz, or with a Keurig 10oz to leave room in your mug, but then you are brewing significantly less coffee than you would get from brewing with 350ml of water.

I love James's content in general but it does feel sometimes a bit like I am being left out of the experience of being in this particular coffee enthusiast community because I have a strong preference for darkly roasted coffee. Around the 14 minute mark he draws a contrast between darkly roasted coffee and "nicely" roasted coffee, implying darkly roasted coffees are bad or being mistreated in the production process. This is not the first time on this channel he implies that darker roasts are failing to live up to what coffee could (or should) be. It honestly sometimes feels like "specialty" is code for "light to medium-light roast" on this channel. The lack of a true dark roast in the Great American Coffee Taste Test reinforced this for me.  I truly like dark roast coffee; I like the earthy tones, I like the notes of cocoa and toffee and wood and caramel. I don't like an acid or fruit forward flavor profile. My daily brew is a Guatemala single origin French roast from a local producer who does a beautiful job crafting their expression of those beans' flavor. I don't really know where I'm going with this, but I do wish that this channel did a better job at recognizing that there is fantastic coffee worth experiencing at every roast level, and that dark roasts don't automatically mean burnt and unpleasant.

Machinist/Mouldmaker and fellow coffee person chiming in on the burr point:  Yes, indeed, manufacturing a proper burr from a hard material like a hardened steel alloy or from a ceramic requires expensive machining time, expense in the material, and expensive cutting tools to cut that hard material. A manufacturer will not take on those costs in a design unless the product was a higher end item. There is simply no way around the costs associated with that critical component. Those false burrs are almost always cast metal or MIM (metal injection moulded): Cheap and fast.  I think this seems like an interesting addition to the space, for someone: Not for me, though. Personally, I prefer making my coffee manually or, when at work we have the serviceable-because-it's-free Keurig.  Cheers!

My experience is the most important thing you can upgrade in your coffee production is the grinder. I was skeptical when I went from a blade grinder to my first relatively cheap burr grinder but was really sold the very first day. As I changed to brewing espresso at home, I upgraded my burr grinder and was very happy with the choice.  Brewing methods have so many relatively inexpensive ways to make coffee. For those, my experience is the more ability to control the process the better. The problem I have in theory is that a machine like this really just has three settings I can change.  An automatic machine is a nice idea, but I routinely adjust grind size depending on different beans and brewing methods, extraction time, dose of coffee, etc. If this is brewing a weaker cup, it might be difficult to adjust things to get an adequate cup. Or it might just be wasteful if I'm having to grind twice the coffee I normally have to.

I think a machine you have missed so far is the Melitta Aroma boy. It is really cheap, below ‚Ç¨50 usually and brews absolutely fantastic coffee. You need to pair it with a grinder, but as budget hand grinders got really cheap lately, it can still be below the ‚Ç¨100 limit. It looks like a toy machine (beige is the only correct color choice :) ), but it brews really good filter. When feeding with good beans it is on par with a v60 brew. Its only drawback is its size: you cannot go above 340 ml brews (that's the capacity of its water tank). Also that while it uses trapezoid filters, it needs a really small size. Few brands have those, so that can be a problem (Cafec has, Melitta only has unbleached). Although can be solved by any trapezoid filter and a pair of scissors.  Because of its small size only a limited amount of brewer can be used instead of the one it comes with. But what you can do is take off it's "lid" (more like a cover, doesn't do anything meaningful) and then you can turn around the spout so it faces back. And then you have a much larger (vertical) space and you can use any brewer or carafe you want. But again, it does a really good job with it's own brewer too so it is not necessary.  I would highly recommend to check it, it's absolutely fantastic for it's price and the brew it produces is on par with the much more expensive coffee machines and even with hand brewing. I think it is the absolutely best beginner brewer, because it is cheap and you can get good brews without any knowledge on brewing.

On the grams/ounces thing: I think a lot of Americans are getting more comfortable using grams to measure small masses, especially in the case of coffee and also the shift towards measuring baking ingredients by mass instead of volume.  On the other hand, the fluid ounce and fractions/multiples thereof works pretty well for small liquid volumes. This doesn't hold up as soon as you need to do more complex math on it, but it honestly makes sense to me.

Example: you will measure out 25 grams of coffee to mix with 470 ml of water. I mix 1 ounce of coffee with 16 ounces of water. which one looks like the unit designed for the task?   Metric is the answer for smug elitist know nothings. Metric is only good at one thing: making convoluted math to figure out theories about things.  the individual systems of measurement used throughout the world came about by people who build things finding a way to measure them practically.   Example: If I am building a door and I need specific lengths of wood for that, it is much better to find out what fraction you need. This is infinitely easier when you use a measurement in base 12 like feet and yards which can be divided by 2,3, or 4 rather easily. In metric it's 5 or 10. Again, this is because people who built things thought up the system that was most useful to them.  And before you even think about it, I have never had to convert feet or yards to miles because there really is no reason to. It is only for math fun to see how many footlong hotdogs you need to circumnavigate the globe at x latitude.  Ounces in coffee are the same way as stated in my first example. It's much easier to give people the choice of 8,12,16,20 oz of coffee than 236.5882375...ml, 354.88235625...ml, 472... And if metric is so wonderful why do you only use ml and L? I never see anything measured in centeliters or decaliters which would seem more appropriate for something the size of a cup of coffee. Why? I thought your system was so wonderful why the unused measures?  No sorry all you non American subjects. Metric is great for mathing out how many newtons of force it takes to overcome the gravitational pull of a x mass black hole with a y mass object. For everyday things like making a cup of coffee, metric is a poor substitute.

I'm not totally against it. When I opened my coffee shop in Poland in 1993, most people thought cappuccino came out of a bag (instant). They had never experienced decent coffee ever in their lives. What I noticed was that some people were able to develop discernment, but there were quite a few who never did. They literally couldn't taste the difference between a cup of instant cappuccino and a real one made from fresh ground espresso and milk.  This machine appears to be made for a very specific audience - North Americans, probably mostly in rural and suburban areas, whose main form of coffee brewing was a filter machine with a carafe, or "no-nonsense" coffee. In other words, the complete opposite end of coffee snobbery. The "Anti-coffee-snob." For them, this might be the first time they try their coffee fresh-ground. That might start to open their eyes to drinking a better cup of coffee or it might not. They're most likely NEVER going to enter the world of espresso or pour-over coffee, and don't want to make the financial commitment to a Keurig machine or the pods (in that sense the metal filter fits right into a certain money-wise mentality).   As far as the "ounces out" aspect, it really wouldn't make sense any other way. People buy cups and mugs in standard sizes (8, 10, 12, 16 oz.) and it would be horribly confusing to choose which setting to use for a given sized cup if the machine were calling out the water-in amount.   I think there's absolutely a market for this machine in the US, and they nailed a product at a price point that that customer will likely agree to spend on it.

James.  Yes, we mix Metric and Imperial.  But you know what?  Fluid measurements are most often made in fl-oz (fluid ounces).  Coffee, which DOES need to be accurately measured, and is most accurately portioned by weight, is done so in grams because dry ounces are typically like 28-30 grams.  There's a method to our madness.

I have learned from years of experience that the old adage, "you get what you pay for," is especially true in the coffee game and even more so in the home roasters game. Bargain basement green coffee beans can only create basement tasting roasted coffee. Cheap made coffee grinders make mostly bad coffee grounds. The burrs make manufacturing so expensive because they're very complex and timey to machine. They're done one at a time and all of that geometry and angling takes quiet a while for a machine to cut and recut and finish cut. I'm not experienced enough with CnC to know how long a process like that would take but I wouldn't be surprised if each burr set takes an hour+ of machine time.
Has any research been done on grinding methods that don't use burrs or blades or any of the ususal suspects? Seems like there is an opportunity to come up with a new design that is none of the above and simpler, cheaper etc. Maybe AI design can do it.

tl;dr: it's funny to think that americans using grams to measure coffee is some victory for metric as a whole in america. It's not and we don't even see it as a competition to be won.  On the imperial vs metric debate I always find it funny how fussed non americans are about what americans use. I get the sense that the rest of the world assumes we're either completely confused or just obstinately anti-metric when that couldn't be farther from the truth. We are very comfortable using either and both and it just comes down to whichever is more convenient for any given situation (with imperial winning out on ties just from old habits). A few mm is more convenient than some fraction of an inch, a few feet is more convenient than some decimal of a meter, a few g is more convenient than some fraction of an ounce, a few fluid ounces is more convenient than some decimal of a liter. The two systems are surprisingly complimentary for day to day use.  Anyway all that to say the fact that we measure our small amounts of coffee in grams is not some "victory" in the "battle" between metric and imperial. It's just one situation where metric is more convenient and we're happy to use it for that reason.
America here. Please bring us Metric, it's much better

I get the impression that this machine is intended for appearances, at the expense of all else. And I don't just mean "looks cute in the kitchen" appearances, but also psychological and social appearances as well. The kind of machine that is bought not for its coffeemaking ability but strictly for its ability to deliver a narrative intended to keep up with the Joneses. I think the brew strength is a giant telltale in the design. That "bold is normal" approach means that practically every person buying this machine will very likely set it to bold simply to drink coffee at a basic level. But what kind of *narrative* does this build? How does this look in front of the friends, the family, and the neighbors? "I drink STRONG coffee, I know what I WANT, I'm a GO-GETTER, I've got the GUMPTION." It plays into the mythical persona that ends their cocktail order with '...and make it strong!' And the insidious part here is that only the machine knows it's lying. The owner doesn't know. The owner can get fooled into believing they like bold coffee ‚Äî and then get a nasty surprise next time they show up in a coffeeshop and are left to wonder if they should question what they received, or try to keep up as a smile as they force the brew down their throats and try not to make a scene about it. This machine, it doesn't matter if the coffee's objectively good or not. I'm willing to bet that this was never a concern at any point in the design phase. All that counts is which buttons its owner pressed to make the coffee, and what kind of personality these button presses translated into to those who were watching. In short, this machine is shallowness personified.
Would love to see more reviews of similar super auto drip machines (not espresso).
About adjusting these fake burrs, I have that melitta grinder that you referenced testing. In an experiment to grind finer, I basically disassembled the top burr from the plastic, cut a piece of cardboard  to shape (of about less than 0.5 mm) and used it as a "washer"/spacer between plastic and burr. As finer grind goes, now what corresponded to 1 in the original scale, is more like 10/11. Word of precaution: I can't grind 5 or below because it jams, maybe a piece of paper would suffice even.  Just thought about mentioning it, in case you want to give it a try, a basically free hacky mod.

Weird opinion here - Fl Oz + Grams is my preferred way of setting an easy repeatable "rule of thumb" for drip machines. Bear with me here, American coffee "cups" are 5 oz, most cheap drip machine manufacturers (at least here) recommend 1 Tbs of beans for each "cup". In my testing, a Tbs is often somewhere around 5g (a "coffee scoop is often around 10g). That's about a 30g/L recipe - which is a little low, but works for some people. If that's the choice, you can literally just go 1-1 water (oz) to grams of beans. For ~60g/L you're looking at essentially twice as much coffee (g) as water (oz). A lot of newer refrigerators have filtered water on the front that count the Fl Oz as they dispense. You can very easily do the math there without weighing your water (and trying to get an awkwardly shaped reservoir to balance on your scale).

Personally, I have a very simple opinion on how to get good coffee. The two most important details to me on how the coffee ends up tasting is the coffee itself (duh) and the grinder. Having a grinder increases the accessibility of specialty coffee, which is usually whole bean. Plus, grinding for just a day or two's worth of coffee ensures it's fresh. A good hand grinder is the cheapest way to get good coffee grounds. So for someone who wants good coffee but doesn't want to change anything at all about their mornings, just get a hand grinder, grind out some coffee and use that coffee in whatever drip machine/french press/moka pot that you use every day. Done deal, your coffee will be 100x better
I could be wrong about the exact model I have (I bought mine in 2020) but I've been using a Cuisinart DGB-400 for a few years now almost every day and it brews great coffee. It uses spinning blades to grind the coffee, and the result is usually pretty consistent (though you can't adjust the size of the grind at all). The buttons are simple enough to use so it doesn't have the weird software issues this DB coffee maker does, and it can brew up to 12 "cups" (really about 6 servings, and the coffee bean hopper would not be able to hold the right amount of coffee beans anyway if I had to guess, but I've had success brewing 10 cups at a time).  It's easy to clean, and because the coffee grinds falls into the mesh filter rather than get flung out they tend to land relatively evenly inside the filter.  For the price point we're pretty happy with it. It's even less expensive now than it was when I first got it (We paid around $120 but it seems to have gone down to $97 on Amazon). We like good-tasting coffee and haven't had any issues with this maker.

Andrew James (who have now gone bust, it was probably someone elses machine rebranded anyway as most of their products were) produced a machine like this, that I used for many years and indeed still have although no longer use.  Primary differences are an adjustable metal grinder (to an extent!) and strength settings, plus it brewed into a Melitta style basket with a nylon filter so could be used with a paper filter if desired.  It brewed in to a glass carafe, could be programmed to make fresh coffee in the morning and the hopper held a standard bag of beans (227g).  Downsides were at max strength it was brewing around 45g a litre, it wasn't quite brewing hot enough (James's old tip of using water from a kettle in the tank helped a bit, but no use for the timer feature) and because the ground coffee comes through a sliding door above the brew basket there was a tendency for dampness and over time the chute could clog with coffee sludge, which was a real PITA to clean.

A note on measurements (hobbyhorse warning): It's incorrect to refer to ounces, in an American context, as "Imperial." The Imperial system of volume measurement was adopted by the British Empire in 1824 (after American independence). It combined elements of the English customary system and a separate Scottish system. This system was not adopted by the United States, which continued (and to this day continues) to use English customary units as defined by the Exchequer Standards of 1588. Notably, the Imperial ounce (28.4131 ml) is smaller than the US ounce (29.574 ml), but the Imperial pint (probably the only measure with relevance in today's Britain) is slightly larger than a US pint, because there are 20 ounces per Imperial pint but 16 ounces per US pint. Imperial gallons are thus larger than US gallons, because there are eight pints per gallon in both systems (actually, four quarts per gallon in the US system, with two pints per quart). It would be more accurate to refer to US measurements as customary (or US) units than as Imperial units.
